<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="26b1487c40684cedb1ae7461722fbfab"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="26b1487c40684cedb1ae7461722fbfab">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '533ffe24-acf3-47f0-abde-30b2fc9fc41c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = 'F416794065EB9ABA56B680FA6AFA51E3';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

                  <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0003-1687-1938"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0003-1687-1938</span></a></li>
               </ul>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d30036475" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>It was discovered that sodium-ion batteries exhibit significantly different gas evolution behavior during discharge compared to the charging gas evolution observed in lithium-ion batteries during formation. This difference primarily arises from the high specific surface area and unique porous structure of the hard carbon anode in sodium ion batteries, which facilitate a specific adsorption-desorption behavior of CO<sub>2</sub> gas.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/82041fd5-3b86-4818-b0ad-7c119ecbee49/anie202412222-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/82041fd5-3b86-4818-b0ad-7c119ecbee49/anie202412222-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6e95a353-2120-4a62-aabd-945706b56af8/anie202412222-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/82041fd5-3b86-4818-b0ad-7c119ecbee49/anie202412222-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d30036478" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>In recent years, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted a lot of attention and are considered an ideal alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). The hard carbon (HC) anode in SIBs presents a unique challenge for studying the formation process of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) during initial cycling, owing to its distinctive porous structure. This study employs a combination of ultrasonic scanning techniques and differential electrochemical mass spectrometry to conduct an in-depth analysis of the two-dimensional distribution and composition of gases during the formation process. The findings reveal distinct gas evolution behaviors in SIBs compared to LIBs during formation. Notably, significant gas evolution is observed during the discharge phase of the formation cycle in SIBs, with higher discharge rates leading to increased gas evolution rates. This phenomenon is likely attributed to the adsorption of CO<sub>2</sub> gas by the abundant pores in HC, followed by desorption during discharge. Furthermore, the study demonstrates that the addition of 5A molecular sieves, which competitively adsorb gases, effectively reduces gas adsorption on the anode during formation, thereby significantly enhancing battery performance. This research elucidates the gas adsorption and desorption behavior at the battery interface, providing new insights into the SEI formation process in SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202412222-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202412222-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the increasing concern about global warming, the energy sector is being forced to innovate by replacing traditional fossil fuels with renewable energy sources.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0001_R_d30036467e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a></sup></span> Consequently, there is an urgent need to establish large-scale electrical energy storage systems (EESs) to store renewable energy sources such as wind and solar power, which are inherently intermittent and variable.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0002_R_d30036467e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span> Over the past few decades, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been considered the most efficient secondary batteries due to their high energy density and long cycle life.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0003_R_d30036467e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">3</a></sup></span> However, the low abundance of lithium resources makes it difficult for LIBs to meet the demands of large-scale energy storage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0004_R_d30036467e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nature">4</a></sup></span> In contrast, sodium resources are extremely abundant.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0005_R_d30036467e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Joule">5</a></sup></span> Therefore, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as promising candidates for EESs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0006_R_d30036467e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a></sup></span> Given the similar chemical properties of lithium and sodium,<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0007_R_d30036467e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Rev.">7</a></sup></span> many design concepts from LIBs have been directly applied to SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0008_R_d30036467e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> These include ion intercalation electrode materials,<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0011_R_d30036467e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span> carbonate-based electrolytes,<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0014_R_d30036467e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci.">10</a></sup></span> and the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0015_R_d30036467e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>However, not all design experiences from LIBs are directly applicable to SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0016_R_d30036467e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> For instance, in the case of anode materials, graphite (Gr) is commonly used in LIBs because Li ions can reversibly intercalate between its layers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0019_R_d30036467e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a></sup></span> However, Gr is unsuitable for SIBs as sodium ions are hard to intercalate into Gr layers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0020_R_d30036467e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">14</a></sup></span> As an alternative, various hard carbon (HC) materials<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0021_R_d30036467e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15">15</a></sup></span> have been developed as anodes for SIBs,<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0024_R_d30036467e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16">16</a></sup></span> achieving relatively high reversible sodium storage capacities.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0027_R_d30036467e497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Energy">17</a></sup></span> Due to the low plateau potential of HC for sodium storage,<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0028_R_d30036467e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span> the electrolyte solvent decomposes on the anode surface during the first chargedischarge cycle.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0029_R_d30036467e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19">19</a></sup></span> During the initial charging process, solvents undergo reduction reactions on the surface of the negative electrode, forming a passivation layer which is called SEI. The formation of the SEI layer is often accompanied by the evolution of gases. Common gases include CO<sub>2</sub>, CO, CH<sub>4</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0030_R_d30036467e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Batteries &amp; Supercaps">20</a></sup></span> Some electrolyte such as EC reacts at the anode surface, producing a number of gases.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0031_R_d30036467e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21">21</a></sup></span> Researchers have investigated the reaction process of related materials. For example, Rodrigo etal. successfully used digital microscopy to visualize gas production during formation in a Na/Na-symmetric optical cell.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0034_R_d30036467e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Energy Lett.">22</a></sup></span> However, the SEI formation process on HC surfaces is not yet fully understood,<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0035_R_d30036467e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23">23</a></sup></span> and many details of the interface formation mechanisms are still unclear.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0038_R_d30036467e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In this study, an abnormal gas evolution phenomenon during SEI formation was reported: a significant release of gaseous byproducts often occurs during the initial discharge process of SIBs, rather than during the initial charge process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0042_R_d30036467e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Sci.">25</a></sup></span> It is generally believed that the increasing battery voltage drives the SEI formation reactions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0043_R_d30036467e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26">26</a></sup></span> These reactions are accompanied by gaseous side reactions,<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0046_R_d30036467e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span> and these reactions should largely complete by the end of the first charge phase. During subsequent discharge cycles, the battery voltage decreases, theoretically halting SEI formation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0047_R_d30036467e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span> Consequently, gas generation should also cease. However, through ultrasonic scanning imaging<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0048_R_d30036467e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span> and differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) techniques,<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0032_R_d30036467e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21a - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">21a</a></sup></span> it was discovered that significant gas release also occurs during the discharge phase in the first cycle of SIBs, and exceeding the total gas release during charging. To explain this phenomenon, a systematic characterization of the gas components produced during formation and the gas adsorption properties of the anode materials was conducted. It was found that the gases produced during the initial charge process are adsorbed by the anode material and then released during the subsequent discharge as the adsorption capacity of the anode material decreases. Based on this mechanism, it is proposed to introduce molecular sieves into the battery, which can competitively adsorb the gases produced during the formation process with the anode material, thereby reducing the adverse effects of gas evolution and significantly improving the battery cycling performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202412222-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202412222-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>In this study, ultrasonic scanning imaging technology<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0049_R_d30036467e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Joule">30</a></sup></span> was employed to analyze the behavior of 1 Ah LIBs and SIBs during the formation process. The cathode material for the LIB was layered LiNi<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (LiNCM523), the anode material was Gr, and the electrolyte composition was 1M LiPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in a solvent mixture of EC:DEC=1:1 (by volume), with 5wt% FEC added. For the SIB, the cathode material was layered NaNi<sub>0.33</sub>Fe<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNFMO), the anode material was HC, and the electrolyte was 1M NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in the same solvent mixture of EC:DEC=1:1 (by volume) with 5wt% FEC added (called NP002), and the schematic diagram of LIBs and SIBs are shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0001">1</a>. During the formation process, the batteries were charged at a constant current (0.2C) and constant voltage until fully charged, then rested for 30minutes, followed by discharging at a constant current of 0.2C.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202412222-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/66435fc5-86d2-4e96-9d1b-d73fca984858/anie202412222-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/66435fc5-86d2-4e96-9d1b-d73fca984858/anie202412222-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d543dfec-2590-40e9-b84b-2229a157fb99/anie202412222-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/66435fc5-86d2-4e96-9d1b-d73fca984858/anie202412222-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202412222-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202412222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Schematic diagram of LIBs and SIBs.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Ultrasonic imaging was performed throughout the formation process, mapping the peak-to-peak value (PPV) of the ultrasonic transmission signal (ranging from 0 to 2500PPV) to a color scale from blue to red. As shown in the scanning results of the LIB in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0002">2a</a>, at the initial stage of charging, except for the internal tab area, the ultrasonic scanning image exhibited strong ultrasonic transmittance (displayed as green). The ultrasonic time-domain signal, shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0002">2c</a>, had a PPV of 1530, indicating good internal wetting of the battery with no significant gas presence.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202412222-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2fe63d27-2cee-4419-9d78-3f3ad6b3a98e/anie202412222-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2fe63d27-2cee-4419-9d78-3f3ad6b3a98e/anie202412222-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f6c7c49b-93bc-4baa-a88f-f540ed85f695/anie202412222-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2fe63d27-2cee-4419-9d78-3f3ad6b3a98e/anie202412222-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202412222-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202412222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Ultrasonic in situ cycle scanning results for LIB (LiNCM523||Gr) (a), and SIB (NNFMO||HC) (b), and ultrasonic transmission signal time-domain waveforms (c, d).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>As the charging process continued and the voltage rose to approximately 3.6V, the internal battery image nearly entirely transformed into a blue region, as shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0002">2d</a>. At this stage, the ultrasonic transmission signal PPV dropped to 60, losing more than 96% of its intensity, indicating the generation of a substantial amount of gas inside the battery. When the battery voltage reached the cutoff voltage of 4.2V, the blue regions decreased, implying that some low acoustic impedance gas was converted into high acoustic impedance substances. This observation aligns with literature reports that gaseous CO<sub>2</sub> in LIBs is absorbed and converted into solid Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> during formation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0050_R_d30036467e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">31</a></sup></span> Upon discharging the battery to the cutoff voltage, the ultrasonic scanning result still showed extensive blue regions, indicating that a significant amount of gas remained within the battery even after the formation process.</p>
         
         <p>The ultrasonic scanning results of the SIB are shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0002">2b</a>. Throughout the charging process, the battery electrode region maintained high ultrasonic transmission, with no areas of low ultrasonic transmission indicative of gas generation. Furthermore, after resting the fully charged battery for 30minutes, no significant blue regions appeared. However, upon discharging the battery, a large number of low ultrasonic transmission areas, represented by extensive blue regions, quickly emerged, indicating substantial gas generation within the battery. Additionally, the battery was tested under different discharge rates (0.5C, 1C) while maintaining the same charging protocol (constant current and constant voltage charging at 0.2C to 100% state of charge (SOC)). The ultrasonic scanning results are shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0003">3</a>. The results indicate that, compared to lower discharge rates (0.2C, 0.5C), the battery exhibited a faster gas generation rate at the higher discharge rate (1C). The attenuation of ultrasound transmission was more pronounced in the centre of the cell than at the edges. This is mainly because we have used two plastic plates to press the cell during the test. When the plates grip the battery, the corners experience greater pressure than the center because the screws are at the corners. Higher pressure leads to tighter contact between electrode particles, which promotes ultrasound transmission. Interestingly, it is generally believed that side reactions occur during the charging phase, particularly at high states of charge (above 80% SOC) and high cutoff voltages,<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0051_R_d30036467e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span> and that side reactions do not occur during discharge. Therefore, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analyses were performed on HC at different SOCs (0%, 25%, 50%, 75%, 100%) during formation charging, as shown in FigureS1. The results indicated that significant changes occurred in the C 1s, O 1s, and Na 1s spectra of HC within the 0%75% SOC range, suggesting the formation of SEIs during this process. In contrast, the C 1s, O 1s, and Na 1s spectra remained largely unchanged from 75% to 100% SOC, indicating that SEIs were primarily formed in the early stages, with no significant side reactions occurring at higher voltages. In addition, the morphology of the electrodes before and after gas production was characterised, as shown in FigureS3 and S4, and the results indicate that there is no change in the morphology and structure of the electrode material before and after the formation. The generation of gas during the discharge phase has not been previously reported in the literature.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202412222-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4b2b3386-2ee3-45c1-a64f-986e9dd3fba7/anie202412222-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4b2b3386-2ee3-45c1-a64f-986e9dd3fba7/anie202412222-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/91df931b-af8a-41a2-ab64-24a1083eb0ca/anie202412222-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4b2b3386-2ee3-45c1-a64f-986e9dd3fba7/anie202412222-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202412222-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202412222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Ultrasonic in situ cycle scanning results for SIB with 0.2C (a), 0.5C (b) and 1C (c) discharge rate.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>DEMS was performed to identify the types of gases generated within the battery. As shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0004">4a</a>, a gas pipeline was added to the gas bag of the pouch cell, and the gas flow was controlled by valves. The gases generated within the battery were transported to the mass spectrometer via the action of carrier gas (FigureS5). By analyzing the mass spectrometry results, the real-time chemical composition and concentration of the gases inside the battery were obtained.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202412222-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ea6a53e1-5fb9-4e11-a009-b94f1a8b46ca/anie202412222-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ea6a53e1-5fb9-4e11-a009-b94f1a8b46ca/anie202412222-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9f38f58a-bddd-48f2-a637-7e0ca88603cd/anie202412222-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ea6a53e1-5fb9-4e11-a009-b94f1a8b46ca/anie202412222-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202412222-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202412222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>DEMS testing of pouch batteries. Schematic diagram of the DEMS test setup for pouch batteries (a). In situ DEMS test results for LIB and SIB (b,c).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The DEMS results of a 1Ah Li-ion pouch battery that was charged to 4.4V at 0.2C constant current and voltage, rested for 10minutes, and then discharged to 3V at 0.2C constant current are shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0004">4b</a>. As the charging and discharging processes proceeded, gases such as CO, C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>, CO<sub>2</sub>, CH<sub>4</sub>, and O<sub>2</sub> began to be produced inside the battery. When the battery voltage rose to 3.2V, the gas generation rate peaked. As the voltage further increased, the gas generation rate gradually decreased. By the end of charging, only a small amount of CO was observed. During the discharge phase, only a small amount of CO gas was detected, and no significant gas generation was observed when the voltage dropped below 4V. Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0004">4c</a> shows the DEMS results for the 1Ah sodium-ion pouch cell that was charged to 4V at 0.2C constant current and voltage, rested for 10minutes, and then discharged to 2V at 0.2C constant. At the beginning of charging, only the CO<sub>2</sub> signal was observed to gradually increase. As the voltage rose, the CO<sub>2</sub> intensity peaked at 2.2V, corresponding to the reductive decomposition of FEC on the anode, and the reaction equation is as follows. The CO<sub>2</sub> signal continued to be detected throughout the charging phase. Notably, at the start of the discharge phase, the CO<sub>2</sub> concentration rapidly increased, peaking at around 3.6V during discharge, and then gradually decreased until the end of discharge. The DEMS scan results for the SIB predominantly showed the generation of CO<sub>2</sub>. Additionally, during the discharge process, the CO<sub>2</sub> curve exhibited small peaks intermittently, which may be due to gas accumulation forming bubbles that intermittently escape into the gas pathway, causing transient increases in CO<sub>2</sub> concentration.<span><sup><a href="#anie202412222-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202412222-bib-0052_R_d30036467e752" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater.">33</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="anie202412222-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2c98ea15-aeca-4ab1-b686-56eccea4b095/anie202412222-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>The DEMS results indicate that gases are generated during the charging phases of both LIB and SIB. To eliminate the interference of gases generated during charging on the ultrasonic scanning results during discharging, LIBs and SIBs were re-vacuum sealed to remove the gases after the initial charge phase. The scanning results are shown in FigureS6. The gas behavior during the charging process of both types of batteries was consistent with the results shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0002">2</a>. After degassing, the ultrasonic scanning images of the LIB remained green during the subsequent discharge process. The only exception was the tab area, and no significant changes in the internal ultrasonic signals were observed until the end of discharge (FigureS6a). This indicates that no gas was generated during the discharge phase of the LIB. As shown in FigureS6b, at the beginning of the discharge phase for the SIB, the internal regions also displayed a uniform green area. Interestingly, only a very small number of regions of low ultrasound transmission (displayed as blue) were observed as the discharge process progressed. This suggests that the gas release during the discharge phase in SIBs mainly originates from the storage of CO<sub>2</sub> generated during the charging process. It is hypothesized that during the formation charging phase, the generation of CO<sub>2</sub> primarily occurs on the surface of the anode. The CO<sub>2</sub> is likely adsorbed by the surface pore structure of the HC, and it does not diffuse into the electrolyte and accumulate there.</p>
         
         <p>To verify this hypothesis, infrared (IR) spectroscopy analysis was performed on HC at different SOC: 0%, 25%, 50%, 75%, and 100%. Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0005">5b</a> presents the ex situ IR spectra results of the disassembled battery anode powders at different SOCs. The IR spectra indicate the presence of CO<sub>2</sub> characteristic absorption peaks at wavenumbers 23352370cm<sup>1</sup>, with the intensity of these peaks increasing at higher SOC. This demonstrates that the CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption capacity of HC increases with higher SOC. Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0005">5c</a> shows the CO<sub>2</sub> temperature-programmed desorption (TPD) results for HC anode powders at different SOCs. Generally, the low-temperature region at about 100C can be attributed to the desorption of physically adsorbed CO<sub>2</sub>. The TPD results reveal that with increasing SOC, the intensity of physical desorption increases, further indicating that the CO<sub>2</sub> physical adsorption capacity of HC is stronger at higher SOC. In the high-temperature region, there is a significant desorption peak for CO<sub>2</sub>, which can be attributed to chemical desorption. Compared to the 0% SOC battery, the charged batteries exhibit a chemical desorption peak around 200C, likely due to the decomposition of the SEI.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202412222-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b20aeccf-2694-47de-a385-ef06b947d513/anie202412222-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b20aeccf-2694-47de-a385-ef06b947d513/anie202412222-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/66fa7c10-c429-410f-be9f-8284ff86ef0a/anie202412222-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b20aeccf-2694-47de-a385-ef06b947d513/anie202412222-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202412222-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202412222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Schematic diagram of CO<sub>2</sub> gas adsorption by HC (a). Non-in situ IR spectra of HC powder at different SOC (b), and CO<sub>2</sub> temperature programmed desorption curves (c). Molecular dynamics modelling of CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption capacity of HC at different Na+ embedding states (d,e,f).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Overall, the analysis indicates that during the formation charging phase, the reduction and decomposition of the solvent on the anode generate CO<sub>2</sub> as the voltage increases. This CO<sub>2</sub> is subsequently adsorbed by the HC. During the discharge phase, the previously adsorbed CO<sub>2</sub> desorbs and accumulates in the electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>Additionally, molecular dynamics simulations were conducted to study the CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption capacity of HC under different states of Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalation. Taking the Gr domain structure in HC as an example, the CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption process at the edge and parallel sides of the graphite laminates was analyzed under three different sodium intercalation states (Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0005">5d</a>,<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0005">e</a>,<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0005">f</a> and FigureS7). the adsorption energy of CO<sub>2</sub> at the edge of the graphite laminates is lower than the positions parallel to the graphitic laminates. This suggests that the primary adsorption sites are not on the planes parallel to the graphite layers but rather at the edge sites. Our hypothesis is that the enhanced adsorption in hard carbon is due to additional defect sites and non-parallel graphite layers. In particular, the adsorption energy of CO<sub>2</sub> on the HC surface increased with the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. This suggests that the higher SOC of the battery, the stronger the CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption capacity of the HC, and vice versa. Therefore, as the discharge process progresses, due to the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, the adsorption energy of the HC anode decreases, leading to CO<sub>2</sub> desorption, causing gas accumulation in the electrolyte, which can be detected by ultrasonic scanning.</p>
         
         <p>The adsorption of gas within the battery can significantly affect the contact between the electrode and electrolyte interface and may lead to the loss of some active sites, thereby impacting the battery performance. To mitigate the adverse effects of gas adsorption during the formation charging process, this study introduced 5A molecular sieves with a strong CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption capacity, and their BrunauerEmmettTeller (BET) testing results are shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0006">6a</a>,<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0006">b</a>. By adding 4wt% of 5A molecular sieve powder to the electrolyte (increasing the battery mass by only 1.2%), the competitive adsorption properties of the molecular sieves can be used to effectively inhibit gas generation during the first cycle (Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0006">6c</a>). This approach is both simple and cost effective. It is worth noting that the presence or absence of molecular sieves has essentially no effect on the impedance of the cell before and after formation (FigureS8). This resulted in a high capacity retention rate of 90% after 200cycles, significantly higher than the 80% capacity retention rate after 140cycles without the molecular sieve addition. The ultrasonic scanning results of the cell without molecular sieves for 20cycles are shown in FigureS10. The results show that the cell started to produce gas at the 10thcycle and the gas inside the cell increased continuously during the subsequent cycles. By the 20thcycle a large amount of gas was already present in the cell. The physical images after cycling, shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0006">6k</a> and Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0006">6p</a>, indicate that the internal gas generation in the battery with the added 5A molecular sieve was significantly less than that without the molecular sieve, leading to reduced gas generation during cycling.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202412222-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6021d01e-e9b2-4cd0-a4ff-68cf9529c4bc/anie202412222-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6021d01e-e9b2-4cd0-a4ff-68cf9529c4bc/anie202412222-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8bd22b22-5a61-4acf-8ea5-87c986d6a7c5/anie202412222-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6021d01e-e9b2-4cd0-a4ff-68cf9529c4bc/anie202412222-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202412222-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202412222" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherm (a) and pore size distribution curve (b) of 5A molecular sieves. Ultrasonic in situ cycle scanning results of SIB with NP002+molecular sieve electrolytes (c). Charge-discharge voltage curves (d, e) and cycling performance (f) in NP002 and NP002+molecular sieve electrolytes. C 1s, O1s, F1s, and Na 1s XPS spectra cycled HC electrode in NP002 (g,h,i,j) and NP002 + molecular sieve electrolytes (l, m, n, o). Photographs of the SIB after 140cycles of NP002 electrolyte addition (k) and after 200cycles of NP002 electrolyte and molecular sieve addition (p).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To understand whether the molecular sieve improved battery performance by enhancing the chemical composition of the SEI and cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI), XPS analysis was performed on the electrodes after cycling. The results are shown in Figure<a href="#anie202412222-fig-0006">6</a> and FigureS11. The HC and NNFMO electrodes cycled in NP002 and NP002 with molecular sieve electrolytes exhibited similar C 1s, O 1s, F 1s, and Na 1s peaks. Detailed XPS fitting peaks and their binding energies are listed in TablesS9 and S10. The XPS results indicate that the addition of the molecular sieve did not affect the chemical composition of the SEI and CEI to improve battery performance. Instead, the enhancement in overall battery performance is achieved by the molecular sieve's effective reduction of internal gas accumulation detrimental to battery performance through its adsorption function.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202412222-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202412222-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>This study systematically investigated the gas evolution behavior of Ah-level SIBs during formation using in situ ultrasonic scanning and DEMS. It was discovered that SIBs exhibit significantly different gas evolution behavior during discharge compared to the charging gas evolution observed in LIBs during formation. This difference primarily arises from the high specific surface area and unique porous structure of the HC anode in SIBs, which facilitate a specific adsorption-desorption behavior of CO<sub>2</sub> gas. The IR and TPD spectroscopy results indicate that during the initial charging process of SIBs, the adsorption of CO<sub>2</sub> on the HC anode increases significantly with the SOC increase. However, during the discharge phase, the desorption of CO<sub>2</sub> from the HC anode occurs and is detected by the ultrasonic scanning. Comparative analysis of gas evolution under different discharge rates revealed that higher discharge rates significantly accelerate the gas generation rate. Importantly, this study effectively reduced gas generation during the formation process by simply adding the gas adsorbent 5A molecular sieve, rather than traditional battery additives. This approach resulted in lower gas generation and higher capacity retention after cycling tests. These findings not only reveal the abnormal gas evolution behavior during the first chargedischarge cycle of SIBs but also elucidate the relationship between gas evolution and battery performance. This understanding is crucial for comprehending the SEI formation process in SIBs, optimizing electrolyte design, and enhancing the overall performance of SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                     <div class="extra-links getFTR"><span class="hidden data-doi">10.1038/35104644</span><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div>
                        <span><a         <script type="text/javascript" nonce="26b1487c40684cedb1ae7461722fbfab">
    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
            const anchor = li.querySelector('a');
            if (anchor && anchor.textContent.trim() === 'Edit Access Denial') {
                li.style.display = 'none';
            }
